RATING: 10
This is by far my favorite social deduction game. Whether 5 or 10 players Avalon has the ability to be fun and exciting throughout the entire time. The more you play the better it becomes as your own group develops a meta and learn more about each other's habits.

To newcomers, the rules can be a bit confusing as there are many similar "phases" to the game (player mission voting, player succeed/fail voting) while having completely opposite mechanics such as the 5th turn rejection "absolute power". The finesse of Avalon only comes out after multiple games and multiple dialogues about the game. 

The inevitable conflict where one day a player does some wacky decision to break the constructed meta, but cannot convince anyone else that it "works" because in reality it may or may not work, and though there is good intention in it, the articulation is never as good as the result which is infected with the great part of Avalon - the unpredictability.

It is, in my groups (which I agree with) THE bluffing and social deduction game which has more elegant and less randomness as the base "The Resistance" game which Avalon stems from.

In the beginning the games will be chaotic, players will screw up or do something that just backfires, but again, the longer Avalon is played - like fine wine, the better it becomes. What makes this game so fun both in and outside of the game is that the conversations about the game - outside of the game - is just as important as what happens inside of it.

RATING: 9
This is a masterclass in lean, devilish game design. And it can fit in a handbag.

Almost nothing happens with the playing pieces. It's all about how players explain those minimal actions in very tense debate around the table.

It is astonishing how much the game challenges players in subtle ways. It tests their ability to lie consistently and their ability to judge exactly when to pass verbal hints. Some players will be on your side and try to help you - but only IF they are able to deduce that you are on their side.

You'll need to back-up your lies with evidence in the form of people's behaviour/choices you remember from previous rounds. So there is a strong-ish 'memory element', which I usually hate in a game, but which creates delicious conflict here. An innocent error can easily be portrayed by others as a lie, so you have to watch your step constantly.

This really does feel like one of those games where adding expansions would stop it being so great. It adds the 'Merlin' and Lady of The Lake elements to its almost-identical The Resistance predecessor, which are excellent, but that is all it needs. 

I can't recommend it highly enough. The only reason I don't give it 10/10 (a score I've only awarded to one game so far) is that it still has that 'love it or hate it' factor. It is possible to go away from the game feeling embarrassed and stupid if things went wrong. Most times, however, it is very rewarding.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
Resistance / Resistance: Avalon
(Played Avalon - but prefer vanilla Resistance)

Replaces Werewolf for the simple fact that in the latter, a player can be eliminated before the first turn, spending the next 45 minutes or so watching the others play.

The Resistance is a lot tighter, has a lot more player interaction and involvement, with just as much tension - if not more - for all involved. The impact of truculent players is greatly lessened in Resistance, whpich can transform a Werewolf session to watching paint dry.

Re-rated: 1 point decrease due to Deception: Murder in Hong Kong. Also, my initial rating may have been inflated by 0.5 points.

RATING: 9.25
The Resistance is a near perfect social deduction game. Few other games can create so much tension.

Unfortunately, I have some minor qualms. The Assassin can win games for free if they get lucky on the Merlin guess. I don't mind it when the Assassin deduces who Merlin is -- this part is fine. The issue is that sometimes it feels like an arbitrary coin toss. Not sure how this could be fixed. The Merlin/Assassin dynamic is interesting enough that the game is better in spite of that luck factor.

Additionally, The Resistance desperately requires everyone at the table to be engaged and aware of all the rules from the get-go. One player who isn't paying attention can easily ruin a game by inadvertently revealing their role or by voting carelessly.

RATING: 7
Probably my favorite hidden role game out there.

UPDATE: After playing it a lot and playing games like Mafia de Cuba and Spyfall, I vastly prefer those two over this one. Dont get me wrong, Avalon is still a fun game, and its definitely a great game to get people interested in Hidden Role games, however I feel like games like Avalon and Werewolf dont give you too terribly much to go on to make any accusations or assumptions. In Mafia de Cuba, as the Don, I can do some mental gymanstics and deduce who may be telling the truth and whos lying based on whatevers left in the box by the time it gets back to me and how many I took out in the beginning, it gives me something to go off of. Likewise, Spyfall, if someone seems like they gave a vague answer, its easier to make assumptions and think they could be the spy. In Avalon, the first 2 rounds are essentially meaningless, even the bad guy will throw in successes every time. It really doesnt start to matter until round 3. So its still a good game, however its no longer my favorite hidden role game.

RATING: 7
I have to admit that I know next to nothing about the world of King Arthur and Merlin. So when this was brought out I was as lost as a possum driven out into the bush and dumped. People were talking about minions of someone? Merlin is good or bad? Who's this other guy? So when we started I cocked up and opened my eyes at the wrong time... restart. On the reset I was still wondering who the hell I was - out shoots my thumb - quick, put it back in, I don't think that's me - I'm sure it was so quick it'll be okay. Luckily it was. Quick, we're playing, I better read the rule book - that'll throw them. (Yes, I have played The Resistance before, but if I only play a game once a year I tend to forget what's what.) So after a round this started to become familiar. After two I get what's going on -  the symbols are different to The Resistance, but it's mostly the same game. People seemed fairly easy to pick and the bad guys chose the wrong Merlin - me. See! I threw them by reading the rule book while we were playing. (I really know how to ruin the fun sometimes.) And for me that's the difference between The Resistance and Avalon - an incompetent player such as myself can do his side a favour and throw the bad guys off Merlin's trail, whereas in The Resistance an incompetent player is just a crap player.

I prefer The Resistance for the theme and the action cards. I was never interested in King Arthur's world.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This one is tough because I think this is a better version of The Resistance. The plot card in the original game were fun but could be very random, and the fact that they were public information often made them hard to play well.

This game does not have the plot cards, and replaces a number of card components with counters (a great thing), but the 'default' Merlin hunt game, I just don't like too much. Don't get me wrong it works just fine, but even with a resistance team playing a perfect game, the spies have a 1 in 3 or 4 chance of winning, just through blind odds. 

The best variant is Excalibur, which is a promo, but you don't really NEED the promo card just the rules. IMO vanilla Resistance + Excalibur is the best way to play, the other modules  such as Merlin or Plot cards, while I will play them and do enjoy them, are fun but at times add too much randomness to a very pure bluffing and deduction game.

RATING: 9.5
This may be the best party game of all time. The accusations, the recriminations, the yelling, the screaming... and it takes a minute to learn the rules. And at that price, I can't think of a game that you can get more playtime out of. It's difficult to find a game that plays 10 gamers well, but this game actually works better at higher numbers... in fact I'd go as far to say that you haven't played it until you've played with 10 and all the extra characters at once.
The fact that you can also give the goodies and the baddies the edge by cherry-picking extras such as Oberon and Morgana really give this game an edge over every other sort of social deduction game that I've come across.

RATING: 9
If you regularly have 6 - 10 people gathered around a gaming table then minimise this window and go order Avalon.

This is social gaming done right... well... that's understating things. This is THE game you want for social relaxed, drinking or non-drinking fun. Non-gamers seem to love it. Gamers seem to love it. EVERYONE seems to love it.

It is awesome. As with Resistance, a little over half the group is panicking and trying to ascertain who amongst them is a spy while the spies are sitting back and plotting the most efficient way of sowing discord, while failing missions and keeping themselves hidden. Avalon throws in a number of roles, the most dynamic of which is 'Merlin'. Merlin knows who the spies are, but cannot give himself away, cause if the spies fail to thwart the Resistance's missions, they can still win if they work out who Merlin is.

Everybody loves this game and, if by some miracle this doesn't end up being your cup of tea, hopefully you can see that everyone should at the very least play try it once.

So much fun, usually gets multiple play throughs and is fun with all different player counts.

RATING: N/A
Excellent party game, ideally for 5 or more (7 or more better).
Each person gets a card, saying they are good or bad. A group then vote of who should go on a mission, the good want it to succeed, the bad want it to fail. Have to try to work out who is good and bad, and identify them. No one dies partway through a game, no moderator required.

New version of original Resistance - King Arthur theme. If I want a game for lots of numbers, this is the best one (ranked no.1 too)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Accessible and fun group deduction game. At the start of the game players are aligned with either those loyal to King Arthur or the Minions of Mordred. A leader is chosen who then has to form a team of a certain number to perform a mission. Secretly, players either chose to succeed to mission or fail the mission and once one side has managed to successfully succeed/fail three missions, they are victorious. The game plays exactly the same way as the original 'The Resistance' with much speculation and accusations on who is aligned with who, which makes for a light-hearted interactive and highly communicative experience. Where the game differs greatly, and where it justifies its existence as a separate publication, is with the introduction of roles, whereby players can play Merlin (who knows who the Minions are), an Assassin (who can kill Merlin at the end of the game if they can deduce who he is), Percival (who knows who Merlin is and can therefore attempt to protect him), and others who add various layers of complexity to the proceedings and seem to interact with at least one other character in some way. With a largish group (between 5 and 10) who are willing to not take things too seriously, this can prove to be a memorable and replayable gaming experience.

RATING: 8
Oh wow this game is good. Been well liked by nearly everyone I played with so far.
This "party" game also does not need a strict party atmosphere. Unlike e.g. "One Night Ultimate Werewolf" which can be a stale experience if not everyone is into actively contributing and talking, this one is more thinky and does not require that. If you want you can also just wait and see for some time. For that reason I would argue that it's a better introductory party game than ONUW. The only time I would prefer ONUW is with smaller kids or reeeeally drunk people.

The only small negative I can see about the game is that after some rounds it can get a bit exhausting to play as Merlin or the bad guys.

As this comprises most of the elements of the normal Resistance and it's expansions in one box and nobody cares about the theme 1 minute into the game anyway, get this one.

RATING: N/A
Brian Crow
Dan Johnson
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Why it is a 7:

Easy to get played: The short play time, the easy rules, and the low profile make this game a really easy one to get to the table.  Whether it is a game or two in the margins of larger events, or a night of constant arguments, this game sees a lot of play.

Deduction: One of the less commonly utilized mechanics in boardgames, deduction mechanics make this game more novel, keeping it from having to compete for its time slot with potential competition.

Teams: Another mechanic that is not added to games often, splitting the players into teams allows them to work cooperatively against an intelligent foe, the best of both worlds.

Why it is not an 8:

Mistake prone: It is not unheard of for people to make movements or noises that divulge their alignment when the minions are supposed to see each other, or throw a fail when they are a loyalist, or accidentally flip their loyalty card onto the floor, or some other mistake that compromises the integrity of the game.  This is probably because of the easy to play nature; people who are tired or inebriate would be up for this where they would avoid something more taxing, and so are more likely to make mistakes.  It isn't a big issue, since the game is so quick to set up and start over again, but it is a problem that is somewhat peculiar to this game (and most social deduction games I assume)

Why it wouldn't be a 9 if it were an 8:

Play is mostly for one team: Most of the interesting choices that emerge during game play really center around the Minions of Mordred team.  Who to put on missions, what to throw on the mission, and whether to pass/fail a mission are all potentially strategic choices that the minions can make.  Loyalists, however, usually have a pretty clear choice based off of the knowledge they have at that time: Either try to put several minions on the same mission or none, don't vote for missions if you think it will fail without giving new information, and you always have to pass the mission.  There are some plays that can be more interesting for the loyalists to make, and the additional roles can spread the decision making love around, but in general, the minions get to make most of the interesting decisions.

Can be powerless to affect the game state: This is another rare occurrence which can color this game's experience.  An example would be 5 players, the minions are sitting next to each other, and the first play goes to the person to the left of the minions.  If that player puts himself and the next person in play order on a team, it will pass.  Then the next person does the same two and adds the next person, it will pass again, then they do the same team again, and it is game over for the minions.  There isn't any real persuasion the minions can attempt to avoid this outcome, because if they were loyalists, they would have just as little information as the other loyalists and there is no reason to distrust anyone at that point. At best, the minions could say that they are a loyalist and therefore know the 3-player team will fail, but that is what a minion would say, isn't it? The problem here is that a minion against a non-novice team who is resistant to emotional appeals could find themselves losing before they had a chance to introduce noise into the loyalists decisions besides the "I know I am a loyalist" line (which should be expected from everyone anyway). 

Why it wouldn't be a 10 if it were a 9:

Advanced play is limited to thwarting the meta:

RATING: 7
This game continues to grow on me and certainly in my game groups, it is easy to pick up but not at all easy to understand strategy or how one should behave. Using more characters definitely makes the game better, although makes thing difficult for newbies.  Fortunately one does not feel like he blew the game since usually it is a team effort and no one person is at fault; so it's well constructed on a lot of levels. The voting is more confusing then it needs to be because there are two sort of votes and they are vastly different, once this difficult concept is grokked the rest is easy to explain (oct '14). 

However, it is better than Resistance in part because you pick more players for the quest and in part because of the characters. If you like Resistance you'll like this even more. 1/25/14

RATING: 6
I really like The Resistance, though am pretty burnt out on it. Avalon adds a 'seer' which I like the idea of, but essentially means two things: 1. The seer must be extremely subtle in convincing the other resistance members as to whom to trust, lest he be found out. Perhaps so subtle that the role becomes useless. This is because the spies then get to pick one person, guess the seer correctly and they win regardless. 2. The spies can now also win through dumb luck. In a 6 player game, they have a 1 in 4 chance of randomly winning the game by guessing correctly. It really shouldn't take any information for them to be able to determine who actually knows who they are...

RATING: 9.9
A more condensed version of lengthier social deduction games such as Werewolf, without the player elimination. While many see this as making it superior, I don't think it carries the complexity and possibilities of Werewolf, but does a good job of capturing it in an easier to play, shorter setting - making it more accessible and more suitable for certain situations and gamer groups. This is a fantastic intro into social deduction, light but still with a lot of depth.

RATING: 10
What I like:

The best social deduction party game! I love being a bad guy trying to hide my identity and tank the missions. I love being a good guy and deductively point out the bad guys. I love having extra information and being powerful like percival. I love trying to play dumb as merlin. All of it is just so fun.
We've had games of Avalon that are still talked about for months and years to come. Sometimes someone is just so damn clever or sneaking and fools the entire table and that is nothing short of epic. No player elimination like warewolf, and the characters are optional if you don't know  if you prefer regular resistance. 


What I dislike:

It seems really really hard for the good guys to win. If the bad guys don't stay hidden for some reason and play too obvious, then Merlin usually have to try and give out some minor hints to his teammates, but none spots these hints like the bad guys cause they are actively looking for them and have a lot more information when interpreting the votes.
Also, you need a big group. It's most fun with 7-10 players and it's very rare I can organize this so I don't get to play it as much as I want.

RATING: 2
Oh, dear god, how I loath this game! It is basically a shouting contest with little to no logic to sustain any argument, for one side or the other. I had a particularly good position to testify this, once I was Merlin, and could see the "reasoning" of the loyal players to find the traitors. One of the player was 100% sure (and he was wrong) that a player was a traitor, and any of my reasons to change this were ignored, and remember I did know in which side was every player. 

Since only a baseball bat would change the mind of some of the loyal players, the loyalist lost 3 to 2 in the missions. But we would also lose by the Assassin, since basically only this other player and I were really talking in the loyal side, and since this other player was wrong, there could be only one option for Merlin. And this is also something I really did not like: even if the traitor do everything wrong, they still have a good shot to try to find Merlin and win the game, by simple luck.

I'm aware that the game can be better with more players and more talkative players in the table. But this experience was so dreadful that I fear I would be hard pressed to say yes to this game again.

I just can't agree that [thing=128882][/thing] or its older brother are [thing=37111][/thing] with all the good parts of this one concentrated in 30 minutes of playing time. How? There isn't all that much at stake - the game is too short for anyone to really care about discovering or not the traitors - I will indeed [i]want[/i] to be right about the roles of everyone, but if you aren't, you just shrug, while in [thing=37111][/thing] the feeling of treason are much bigger, the betrayal cuts way deeper, because you are invested in the game, you see the resources going down, the failed crises, you look the dire space battles, so everything is more colorful, with more at stake.

Sure, [thing=128882][/thing] is a game that can have room for up to 10 players, and it can be played relatively fast (though the arguments can extend), but even in its small time, I don't feel at all rewarded for playing it. The little I used was just wasted.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a game where I have literally never witnessed a bad game. Only reason for not giving this game a 10 is that sometimes the theme can be a bit too "fancy" for some non-gamers so they might shy away from it. The roles are fantastic and are the reason why our group can easily play this game ten times in a row (atleast percival, merlin, morgana and mordred should be included literally every game even with only 5 people). The other reason is that the game is very fast so losing does not feel too bad. I highly recommend to play with Excalibur since it adds a lot to the game (you don't need the expansion for it, just use some plastic sword as an Excalibur and it works perfectly). It is also extremely important that as a host you use some time to explain the idea of the roles (percival should act like merlin etc..) and some general strategy (don't always vote yes for the suggested team etc).

RATING: 7
I like this version way more. For one thing, the art actually looks better. The bad guys look very distinct from the evil traitors. It is easier to understand that there are evil knights in King Arthur's court. Compare this to the futuristic Resistance where the roles are a little more confusing since the traitors are actually government agents. The art work is also less clear cut. I know these comments are just nitpicky given gameplay is almost exactly similar, but Avalon gets a thumbs up from me. 

Still, I already own the regular old resistance and it is hard to justify getting a brand new copy.

RATING: 9
A bluffing game of the werewolf variety where several players are spies, and the group tries to figure out who the spies are and who the loyal resistance members are. Lots of deductive reasoning, plus bluffing. 

Avalon is just the original The Resistance game with some extra optional expansion cards added. Get this and not the original game, as The Resistance had optional cards that didn't work well; whereas the optional expansion cards in Avalon do work well, though I like to just play the base game without them most of the time.

I prefer One Night Ultimate Werewolf to this game, but if you like either one, I suggest you get both, as they are much different, even though they are the same kind of game. Also, I highly recommend Coup if you like this game, as it's also the same type of game yet unique.

RATING: 10
I am a great fan of 'Avalon', as many will know, as it is a very simple but subtle conspiracy/hidden identity themed game which has loads of interaction and absolutely no downtime. You are either allied to Arthur, King of the Britons (the 1970's HTV TV series theme tune hums away in the background) or Mordred and Morgana, Arthur's illegitimate son and shadowy half sister, respectively. Five quests must be completed, and each turn, one player in clockwise order becomes King Arthur, with the responsibility of selecting his most 'faithful' knights to complete these quests successfully. There difficulty increases, as the preceding one is resolved, and King Arthur and his knights must chose wisely. Since each player has a hidden identity - they may be loyal or disloyal - players must try to analyse who is on their side. The servants of Mordred know who undermines the King with them, and if Merlin is played, he knows their identities, whereas they do not know his! Playing Merlin in 'Avalon' is a difficult call, as I rediscovered first time back, so Pete Myatt faced Hobson's (not Sophie's) Choice in the second game as had he not selected the perfect team, King Arthur would have lost his throne. Careless. Mordred and his supporters must be wary of both failing a quest, if selected at the same time, so always watch out for any comments that may give clues as to how players are looking to vote! Games can be made more interesting with the addition of other characters, such as Morgana and Mordred themselves, Oberon, Percival and the Assassin. In the basic game, only Merlin and the assassin are included. In the event that the loyal knights win, the servants of Mordred may try to guess the identity of Merlin, and if they are correct, they win. The assassin player chooses, ultimately, but may take advice from the other Mordred supporters, or not, as they see fit. Of the three minions of Mordred, only one, and not the assassin, would have pegged me for Merlin in the first game, but it was academic. Was I too enigmatic or too shadowy? That's the fun of it! I need to improve my observational skills as to other people's behaviours on voting. It was good to see, after many promptings by me, that 'Avalon' has returned and I will continue to promote its adoption more regularly.

RATING: 10
We never really liked the special abilities in The Resistance, preferring to just play it vanilla, so I was pretty wary going into Avalon.  Turns out, it's pretty much genius.  The core change is Merlin, which is just an incredibly subtle and well-designed role.  The tension is absolutely perfect--he knows, he wants to help the team win, but if he's obvious about it the team will lose.

There seems to be a bit less full-out arguing so far in this than in standard Resistance, since more people are in the know each round.  Then again, that could just be because we haven't found our groove yet. :)

Overall, huge thumbs up.

RATING: 3
If you like the theme of a traitor amongst your group in a game, I do NOT recommend Avalon.

This 'game' is not what I would consider a 'game'. It's just a deduction activity that I don't find very much fun in it(And I enjoy deduction games if done right). It's just deduce and find the traitor. When you play not everyone gets to do something because if people suspect you to be the bad guy, not let you join in on missions, you don't really get to contribute other than trying to convince the others to let you join.
Also you need to play with a good group of people or else this will turn into an unpleasant experience.

It's not like other games with traitor elements like Battlestar galactica where there's a main game to play through, but there's a traitor element that's a part of the game that can be in it if you want(Though highly recommended).

The only positives I can say for this 'game' is that it's fast, and a lot of players can play. But you could play this game with playing cards by having certain colors and suits dictate which good guy or bad guy you are.

So if you're like me, I don't recommend Resistance or any of it's variant.

RATING: 10
This is my favorite game, and the game I play most often. It does have a bit of a learning curve, and new players may be a bit overwhelmed if playing with experienced opponents.  But if you learn the game's intricacies and conflicting objectives, your efforts will be rewarded.  Every character has their own goals and challenges to keep track of, even generic blue (base character) is a critical to their team's success.  The game play mechanics are quite simple, but don't confuse this with a simple game. The game's depth and complexity lies in the way all of the asymmetric information is distributed, shared, and used against the people who have it. The game is impecabally balanced with good outnumbering evil, but evil has more information and an easier time creating failures. The game plays well with any number of players from 5-10, but definitely changes and becomes more olcomplex with more players. I can't recommend this game enough.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another variation on Werewolf, but well done in that it gives nearly all players something interesting to do for the whole game. Plays quickly, ideal for multiple plays.

Favourite combination of special characters is probably Merlin, Assassin, Percival, Morgana, Mordred. Not a fan of Excalibur or Lady of the Lake; haven't tried Oberon/Lancelot enough to judge those two.

Enjoyment will depend largely on the group playing it; groups with a dominating player or players that don't like to talk bullshit and deception probably won't enjoy it as much. Also, if you get stuck in a groupthink/convention rut it might get stale (the meta-game changes with repeated play, or at least it should).

RATING: 6
This game is tough to review. 
In it self it is actually a quite good game that we feel is balanced from 5 players all the way up to 10.
The problem here is that if you, like us, usually play with the same group of people then you will start to learn how to read them. This kinda breaks the game hence the low rating.
In the beginning it was quite nice, but now I'd rather leave it on the shelf unless it is with a group of people who have not played it before.
